Observational Evidence and Consensus on Sphericity and Centrality
The author presents observational proofs and then a caveat, ending with consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will recognize the observational evidences cited for the sphericity of water and earth, and understand the caveat that these observations do not conclusively prove roundness without excluding other causes.

Core Idea
Observations support but do not strictly prove sphericity.

After presenting the rational proofs, the author turns to additional empirical evidence. He discusses how sailors see mountain tops before their bases, how stars appear similarly sized across regions, and how star risings show a consistent ratio — all indicating a spherical earth and water. He then cautions that these observations, while suggestive, do not yield certainty about actual roundness and centrality if other causes are not ruled out. Finally, he notes the consensus of investigators.

Guided Path
1
Read the observational evidence
There are three observations: water sphericity, earth centrality, earth sphericity.
Notice the logic behind each.
2
Examine the water evidence
Seeing mountain tops before their bases from a ship implies curved water surface.
This is analogous to modern horizon evidence.
3
Examine the star evidence
Stars appear same size everywhere (centrality) and their movements are consistent (sphericity).
4
Understand the caveat
These observations do not conclusively prove roundness unless other causes are ruled out.
The author is cautious.
5
Note the consensus
Despite the caveat, the investigators agree on sphericity and centrality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال والعمدة في كرية (الكل) بساطتها وفي كون الأرض في الوسط، ثقلها المطلق، وفي سكونها أنها في حيزها مع انتفاء الميل المستدير لوجود المستقيم، وقد يستدل على كرية الماء برؤية السائر في البحر أعالي الجبال قبل حضيضانها، وعلى توسط الأرض بعدم تفاوت الكواكب في الصغر والكبر بحسب البقاع، وعلى كريتها بكون طلوع الكواكب وغروبها، وارتفاع القطب وانخفاضه على نسبة واحدة بحسب البقاع وكأنه تحدث، وإلا فبعد تسليم انتفاء سبب آخر لا يفيد حقيقة الاستدارة والتوسط. = قد اتفق المحققون على أن العناصر كلها كرية الشكل، وأن الأرض في
He said: The main proof for the sphericity of all [elements] is their simplicity, and for the earth being in the center is its absolute heaviness, and for its stillness is that it is in its place with the absence of circular inclination due to the presence of straight inclination. And one may infer the sphericity of water from the observer at sea seeing the tops of mountains before their bases, and the centrality of the earth from the absence of variation in the size of stars according to regions, and its sphericity from the rising and setting of stars, and the elevation and lowering of the pole being in a fixed ratio according to regions, as if it were a [؟] . Otherwise, after conceding the absence of another cause, it does not yield the reality of roundness and centrality. = The investigators have agreed that all elements are spherical in shape, and that the earth is in [the center].
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage presents the main proof (العمدة) for the sphericity of the celestial and elemental bodies and the earth's central position. The author states: For the sphericity of all, the proof is their simplicity (بساطتها). For the earth being in the center, the proof is its absolute heaviness (ثقل المطلق). For its stillness, the proof is that it is in its natural place, lacking circular inclination because straight inclination (downward motion) is present. Additional observational evidence follows: the sphericity of water is inferred from seeing mountain tops from a ship before their bases; the earth's centrality is inferred from the uniform size of stars across regions; and the earth's sphericity is inferred from the consistent ratios of star risings, settings, and pole elevation across regions. The author notes that if no other cause is admitted, these observations do not conclusively prove actual roundness and centrality. Finally, he reports the consensus of investigators that all elements are spherical and that the earth is at the center (the sentence is incomplete, presumably continuing 'في الوسط').

Key Terms
كرية
استدلال
وسط
كوكب
قطب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Observations alone are sufficient proof for sphericity.
Correction: The author says they are not conclusive unless other causes are ruled out.

Checkpoint
What does the observation of seeing mountain tops before their bases from a ship infer?
A) The earth's centrality
B) The sphericity of water
C) The stillness of earth
D) The simplicity of elements

Key Takeaway
Observational evidence supports but does not conclusively prove sphericity and centrality; rational proof and exclusion of alternatives are needed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that even after conceding no other cause, the observations 'do not yield the reality of roundness.' How confident are you in the conclusions you draw from your own everyday observations about Allah's creation? What would it take for you to move from mere inference to certainty?
?
The author reports a consensus of investigators. Are you someone who accepts a truth simply because many experts agree, or do you demand a rational proof for yourself? Which brings you closer to Allah—relying on authority or seeking personal conviction?
?
Imagine you are that sailor at sea seeing the mountain tops first. Your sight tells you the water is curved. But the author warns that this alone is not proof. When it comes to matters of faith, how often do you mistake a strong impression for certain knowledge? What step can you take today to examine one of your deeply held assumptions?

Consensus and Two Types of Proofs
Sanusi states consensus and introduces two proof types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus among investigators that the elements are spherical and the earth is central, and the distinction between educational (ānīya) and natural (limīya) proofs.

Core Idea
The earth's sphericity is agreed upon, but proofs differ: those showing 'that it is' vs. 'why it is'.

The passage opens with a strong consensus statement: all elements are spherical and the earth is at the center, stationary. It then introduces two fundamental types of proof used in astronomy and natural philosophy. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the consensus statement
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning to understand the agreement on earth's sphericity, centrality, and immobility.
Notice the precise definition of 'center' relative to the celestial sphere.
2
Identify the two proof types
Look for the terms 'educational' (تعليمي) and 'natural' (طبيعي) proofs. Note what each type is said to establish: existence (آنية) vs. causality (لمية).
These are technical terms in Islamic philosophy.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask why the author distinguishes these two types. Consider how different proofs serve different purposes in establishing truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد اتفق المحققون على أن العناصر كلها كرية الشكل، وأن الأرض في وسط بمعنى أن وضعها من السماء كمركز الكرة عند محيطها، وأنها لا تتحرك لا من المركز. ولا إليه ولا عليه واستدلوا على ذلك بحسب النظر التعليمي بأدلة مذكورة في كتب الهيئة تفيد الآنية، وبحسب المنظر الطبيعي بما يفيد اللمية على ما ذكر في علم السماء والعالم مثل أن العناصر بل الفلكيات بسائط ، والشكل الطبيعي للبسيط هو الكرة ، لأن مقتضى الطبيعة الواحدة لا يختلف، وأن الأرض ثقيل مطلق، فتكون تحت الكل، وهو ما يلي مركز محدد الجهات، وإذا كانت في حيزها الطبيعي لم تتحرك عنه، ولا إليه. وإن في الأرض مبدأ ميل مستقيم على ما يرى في أجزائها، فلا يكون فيها مبدأ ميل مستدير، لتضاد الميلين، فلا تتحرك على المركز كما ذهب إليه البعض من أن ما يظهر من الطلوع والغروب بالحركة اليومية مستند إلى حركة الأرض على مركزها حركة وضعية من المغرب إلى المشرق والكل ضعيف، لأنها لا يفيد كونها كذلك في الوجود، لأن مقتضى الطبع قد يزول بالقاسر، فيجوز أن لا تبنى على الكرية، ولا في الوسط، وتتحرك على الاستدارة لا بالطبع كالفلك. وأما الأدلة التعليمية فكثيرة مذكورة في موضعها بما عليها من الإشكالات مثل استدلالهم على كرية الماء بأنه لو لم يكن كرياً ساتراً بنفسه، لأسافل الجبل الشامخ على ساحل البحر ، لظهر الجبل كله دفعة للسائر في البحر، وليس كذلك لأنه يظهر له رأس الجبل أولا ثم ما تحته قليلاً قليلاً، ويتحقق ذلك، بأن توقد نيران على مواضع مختلفة من أعلى الجبل إلى أسفله. ومثل استدلالهم على كون
The investigators have agreed that all elements are spherical in shape, and that the earth is in the center, meaning its position from the heaven is like the center of the sphere from its circumference, and that it does not move from the center or to it or upon it. And they proved that according to educational consideration with proofs mentioned in books of astronomy that yield that it exists (al-āniyyah), and according to natural consideration with what yields causal explanation (al-limiyyah) as mentioned in the science of the heaven and the world, such as that the elements, indeed the celestial bodies, are simple, and the natural shape of a simple body is the sphere, because the requirement of a single nature does not differ, and that the earth is absolutely heavy, so it is below everything, which is adjacent to the center of defined directions, and when it is in its natural place it does not move from it or to it. And that in the earth there is a principle of straight inclination as seen in its parts, so there is no principle of circular inclination in it, due to the opposition of the two inclinations, so it does not rotate on its center as some have claimed that what appears of rising and setting through daily motion is due to the earth's motion on its center as a rotational motion from west to east, and all of that is weak, because it does not prove that it is so in existence, because the requirement of nature may be removed by coercion, so it is possible that it is not based on sphericity nor in the center, and that it moves circularly not by nature like the celestial sphere. As for educational proofs, they are many and are mentioned in their proper place with the objections against them, such as their inference for the sphericity of water: if it were not spherical covering itself, the lower parts of a lofty mountain on the seashore would appear all at once to the sailor, but it is not so, because the top of the mountain appears first, then what is below it little by little, and that can be verified by lighting fires at different points from the top of the mountain to its bottom. And like their inference for the ...
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage first states the consensus of investigators that all elements are spherical and that the earth is in the center, defined as the center of the celestial sphere. It then presents two types of proofs: educational (تعليمي) proofs, which demonstrate existence (آنية) and are found in astronomy books, and natural (طبيعي) proofs, which demonstrate causality (لمية) as in natural philosophy. The natural proofs include: (1) elements and celestial bodies are simple, and the natural shape of a simple body is the sphere because a single nature has a uniform requirement; (2) the earth is absolutely heavy, so it must be below everything, which is adjacent to the center; (3) when in its natural place, it does not move from it or to it; (4) the earth has a principle of straight inclination, as seen in the fall of its parts, so it cannot have a principle of circular inclination, because the two inclinations are opposed. Therefore, the earth cannot rotate on its center as some claim. The author then criticizes this natural argument, noting that it is weak because the requirement of nature may be overridden by coercion, so it is possible that the earth is not spherical, not in the center, and moves circularly by force like the celestial sphere. Then the passage turns to educational proofs, giving one example: the sphericity of water is inferred from the gradual appearance of a mountain to a sailor as he approaches — if water were not spherical, the entire mountain would appear at once. This can be verified by lighting fires at different altitudes on the mountain. The passage breaks off at another example.

Key Terms
العناصر
آنية
لمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What do educational proofs (أدلة تعليمية) establish, according to the passage?
A) Causal explanation (لمية)
B) Existence (آنية)
C) Sphericity of all elements
D) Consensus of investigators

Key Takeaway
The consensus on earth's sphericity is supported by two logically distinct kinds of evidence: one showing that it is so, another explaining why.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn about a core Islamic belief, do you stop at knowing it is true (آنية) or do you also seek to understand the wisdom behind it (لمية)? In what area of your faith have you settled for only one?
?
The passage shows that qualified scholars (المحققون) reached consensus based on distinct proofs. In your own religious practice, do you accept conclusions on authority alone without examining the proofs? How will you change this starting today?
?
Consider the earth's sphericity—a truth you likely accept without personal verification. Is there a truth in your aqeedah that you hold only by imitation? What step will you take to personally engage with its evidence?

Natural Proofs and Their Weakness
Sanusi presents natural proofs and then critiques them as weak.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the natural arguments for the earth's sphericity, centrality, and rest, and why they are considered weak due to the possibility of coercion.

Core Idea
Natural proofs rely on nature, but nature can be forced — so they are not conclusive.

The author presents a series of natural proofs based on the simplicity of elements, the absolute heaviness of earth, and the presence of straight inclination. However, he immediately critiques them, pointing out that nature can be forced, making these proofs insufficient to establish the actual state of the earth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three natural proofs
Read the passage and list the three arguments: simplicity leads to sphericity, heaviness leads to centrality, straight inclination prevents rotation.
Each argument builds on the nature of simple bodies and their tendencies.
2
Understand the critique
The author says these proofs are weak because natural tendencies can be overridden by coercion (القاسر). Find this statement and understand its implication.
This critique undermines the certainty of natural arguments.
3
Reflect on the alternative possibility
Consider that the earth could be non-spherical, non-central, and rotating by force, like the celestial sphere.
This shows the limits of natural philosophy in establishing absolute truths.

Educational Proof: Sphericity of Water
An educational proof for water's sphericity via mountain visibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an example of an educational proof for the sphericity of water, based on the gradual appearance of mountains to sailors.

Core Idea
If water were flat, a mountain would appear all at once — but it appears gradually, proving curvature.

Educational proofs are based on observable phenomena. One such proof for the sphericity of water comes from the behavior of mountains as seen from the sea. The author describes how the top of a mountain appears first, then the lower parts, which would not happen if the water's surface were flat.

Guided Path
1
Visualize the scenario
Imagine a sailor approaching a mountain on the coast. Consider what would be seen if the water surface were flat vs. curved.
The key is the gradual appearance of the mountain from top to bottom.
2
Understand the logical inference
The passage argues that if water were not spherical, the entire mountain would appear at once. Since it appears gradually, water must be spherical.
This is an educational (تعليمي) proof because it uses observation to establish existence.
3
Note the verification method
The author suggests lighting fires at different altitudes on the mountain to observe their appearance from the sea.
This shows the empirical nature of educational proofs.

This passage first states the consensus of investigators that all elements are spherical and that the earth is in the center, defined as the center of the celestial sphere. It then presents two types of proofs: educational (تعليمي) proofs, which demonstrate existence (آنية) and are found in astronomy books, and natural (طبيعي) proofs, which demonstrate causality (لمية) as in natural philosophy. The natural proofs include: (1) elements and celestial bodies are simple, and the natural shape of a simple body is the sphere because a single nature has a uniform requirement; (2) the earth is absolutely heavy, so it must be below everything, which is adjacent to the center; (3) when in its natural place, it does not move from it or to it; (4) the earth has a principle of straight inclination, as seen in the fall of its parts, so it cannot have a principle of circular inclination, because the two inclinations are opposed. Therefore, the earth cannot rotate on its center as some claim. The author then criticizes this natural argument, noting that it is weak because the requirement of nature may be overridden by coercion, so it is possible that the earth is not spherical, not in the center, and moves circularly by force like the celestial sphere. Then the passage turns to educational proofs, giving one example: the sphericity of water is inferred from the gradual appearance of a mountain to a sailor as he approaches — if water were not spherical, the entire mountain would appear at once. This can be verified by lighting fires at different altitudes on the mountain. The passage breaks off at another example.

Key Terms
كرية الماء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What observable phenomenon is used to prove the sphericity of water?
A) The reflection of the sun on water
B) The gradual appearance of a mountain from the sea
C) The circular shape of the horizon
D) The movement of tides

Key Takeaway
The gradual appearance of mountains from the sea provides an educational proof that water has a curved, spherical surface.

?
The proof uses a simple, repeatable observation to establish truth. Is there a belief in your faith that you hold only because you were taught it, without having personally reflected on evidence from the world? What would it take for you to seek that evidence?

If you were to light fires on a mountain and watch them appear one by one, would you see it as mere coincidence or as a sign of a deliberate order? How can you cultivate a heart that sees every natural phenomenon as pointing back to its Creator?

Condition for Valid Causal Inference
The author refutes an argument about causation and insists on excluding other causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why inferring a specific cause from an effect requires excluding all other possible causes, and why sensory observation alone is insufficient.

Core Idea
Inferring a cause from an effect requires excluding all other possible causes.

In this passage, the author refutes a rival argument that attempts to prove a specific cause from the existence of an effect. He points out a critical gap: such an inference only holds if you have already ruled out any other possible cause. If you haven't, then your reasoning amounts to mere conjecture, not logical proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the compressed argumentative style.
The passage is dense but every phrase matters.
2
Identify the condition for valid inference
Look for the phrase 'and it is not complete unless the negation of another cause is demonstrated.' This is the core claim: an effect points to a cause only if all other causes are excluded.
This is the logical requirement the author insists upon.
3
Understand the critique of sensory inference
The author argues that even if the condition were granted, the opponent's inference only yields circularity or intermediacy in a sensory sense, not in reality. Ask: what does 'according to sense, not reality' mean?
Sensory observation (e.g., moon and sun) gives correlation but not necessary causation.
4
See the conclusion: conjecture
The author concludes that the only escape is to admit the inference is based on conjecture (tahaddus). Reflect on how this undermines the opponent's certainty.
Conjecture is not proof in kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبنياً على سبب آخر غير الاستدارة والتوسط، وحاصله أن ما ذكرتم استدلال بوجود المسبب على وجود سبب معين، ولا يتم إلا إذا بين انتفاء سبب آخر. ولو سلّم، فما ذكر لا يفيد إلا الاستدارة والتوسط بحسب الحس دون الحقيقة، ولا محيص إلا بالرجوع إلى أن ذلك تحدس كما في استضاءة القمر بالشمس.
Based on another cause other than circularity and intermediacy, and its summary is that what you mentioned is inference from the existence of the effect to the existence of a specific cause, and it is not complete unless the negation of another cause is demonstrated. And if it is conceded, what is mentioned does not yield anything except circularity and intermediacy according to sense, not reality, and there is no escape except to refer to that being conjecture, as in the illumination of the moon by the sun.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is the end of a refutation. The author is responding to an opponent's argument that infers a specific cause from an effect. He says that such an inference only works if all other causes are excluded. If that condition is not met, the inference leads only to circular reasoning (al-istidara) and intermediacy (al-tawassut) in a sensory sense, not in reality. The only way out is to admit that the inference is based on conjecture (tahaddus), analogous to how we infer the moon's light comes from the sun—through habitual observation, not logical necessity.

Key Terms
سبب (cause)
مسبب (effect)
استدارة (circularity)
توسط (intermediacy)
تحدس (conjecture)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Observing a correlation between two events (e.g., the moon’s light and the sun) is sufficient to prove that one causes the other.
Correction: Correlation does not prove causation; other possible causes must be ruled out. Without that, the inference is mere conjecture.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is required for an inference from an effect to a specific cause to be valid?
A) The existence of the effect
B) The negation of any other cause
C) Sensory observation
D) Conjecture

Key Takeaway
An inference from an effect to a specific cause is only valid if all other possible causes are excluded; otherwise, it is mere conjecture.

How often do you mistake a constant conjunction (like your actions and their results) for a necessary connection, forgetting that Allah is the independent agent? What practical step can you take today to shift from conjecture to tawheed-based certainty?

Formation of Frost, Dew, and Rain Clouds
Explains physical causes of frost, dew, and rain clouds from vapor condensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Islamic natural philosophy explains the formation of frost, dew, and rain clouds through vapor condensation and obstacles.

Core Idea
Frost and dew are the frozen and unfrozen versions of the same process as snow and rain.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid explores the physical causes behind everyday weather phenomena. Before examining the role of smoke in thunder and lightning, the text first establishes how frost, dew, and rain clouds form from vapor under different conditions. You will see how the author uses analogy and cause-effect reasoning rooted in Aristotelian physics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic excerpt once, then the translation. Notice the compact style and the use of analogy.
The passage is the first part of a longer explanation.
2
Analyze the analogy
Identify the relationship between frost and dew and snow and rain. The author states the ratio is the same.
This establishes a conceptual equivalence.
3
Understand rain cloud formation
Note the two conditions: abundant vapor and cold, but without reaching the frigid zone due to an obstacle.
The obstacle prevents the vapor from rising high.
4
List the obstacles
Identify the specific obstacles mentioned: winds, mountains, and the weight/slowness of the vapor itself.
These are physical causes within an Aristotelian framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن انجمد نزل صقيعاً، وإلا فطلا، فنسبة الصقيع إلى الطل، نسبة الثلج إلى المطر، وقد يكون السحاب الماطر من بخار كثير يتكاثف بالبرد من غير أن يتصعد إلى الزمهريرية، لمانع مثل هبوب الرياح المانعة للأبخرة من التصاعد، أو الضاغطة إياها إلى الاجتماع، بسبب وقوف جبال قدام الرياح، ومثل ثقل الجزء المتقدم وبطء حركته، وقد يكون مع البخار المتصاعد دخان، فإذا ارتفعا معاً إلى الهواء البارد، وانعقد البخار سحاباً واحتبس الدخان فيه، فإن بقي الدخان على حرارته قصد الصعود، وإن برد قصد النزول. وكيف كان فإنه يمزق السحاب تمزيقاً عنيفاً، فيحدث من تمزيقه ومصاكته صوت هو الرعد، ونارية لطيفة هي البرق، أو كثيفة هي الصاعقة، وقد يشتعل الدخان الغليظ بالوصول إلى كرة النار، كما يشاهد عند وصول دخان سراج منطفىء إلى سراج مشتعل فيسري فيه الاشتعال، فيرى كأنه كوكب انقض وهو الشهاب، وقد يكون الدخان لغلظه لا يشتعل، بل يحترق ويدوم فيه الاحتراق، فيبقى على صورة ذوابة أو ذنب أو حية أو حيوان له قرون وربما يقف تحت كوكب، ويدور مع النار بدوران الفلك إياها، وربما تظهر فيه علامات هائلة حمر وسود بحسب زيادة غلظ الدخان، وإذا لم ينقطع اتصال الدخان من الأرض، ونزل اشتعاله إلى الأرض يرى كأن تنيناً ينزل من السماء إلى الأرض وهو الحريق.
If it freezes, it descends as frost; otherwise as dew, so the ratio of frost to dew is the ratio of snow to rain. And the rain cloud may be from abundant vapor that condenses due to cold without ascending to the frigid zone, due to an obstacle such as winds that prevent vapors from rising, or that compress them to gather, because of mountains standing before the winds, and such as the heaviness of the preceding part and the slowness of its motion. And there may be smoke accompanying the rising vapor, so when they both rise together into the cold air, and the vapor condenses into a cloud and the smoke is trapped in it, then if the smoke retains its heat, it aims to ascend, and if it cools, it aims to descend. In any case, it tears the cloud violently, and from its tearing and friction a sound occurs—that is thunder, and a fine fiery [substance]—that is lightning, or a dense one—that is the thunderbolt. And the thick smoke may ignite upon reaching the sphere of fire, as is observed when the smoke of a snuffed-out lamp reaches a burning lamp and the flame spreads into it, so it appears as if a falling star has fallen—that is the meteor. And the smoke, due to its thickness, may not ignite, but rather burns and combustion persists in it, so it remains in the form of a tail or a mane or a snake or an animal with horns, and perhaps it stops beneath a star, and rotates with the fire by the rotation of the celestial sphere, and perhaps there appear in it terrifying signs, red and black, according to the increase in the thickness of the smoke. And if the connection of the smoke from the earth is not cut off, and its ignition descends to the earth, it appears as if a dragon is descending from the sky to the earth—that is the fire.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage provides a natural philosophical explanation for various atmospheric and celestial phenomena. It begins by explaining the formation of frost and dew, analogizing the ratio of frost to dew as that of snow to rain. It then describes how rain clouds can form from abundant vapor that condenses due to cold without reaching the frigid zone, due to obstacles like winds or mountains. The text then introduces the role of smoke accompanying rising vapor. When both vapor and smoke rise into cold air, the vapor condenses into a cloud and the smoke is trapped. If the smoke retains heat it rises, if cooled it descends, and in either case it violently tears the cloud, producing thunder from tearing and friction, lightning as a fine fiery substance, and the thunderbolt as a dense one. Next, it explains that thick smoke may ignite upon reaching the sphere of fire, analogous to a snuffed-out lamp wick relighting from a burning flame, and this appears as a falling star or meteor. If the smoke is too thick to ignite entirely, it undergoes sustained combustion, taking forms like a tail, mane, snake, or horned animal, and may appear to rotate with a star or produce red and black signs depending on smoke density. Finally, if the smoke's connection to the earth is continuous and its ignition descends, it appears as a descending dragon—that is, a fire from the sky.

Key Terms
صقيع
طل
ثلج
مطر
سحاب
بخار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Frost and dew are entirely different phenomena with separate causes.
Correction: The author states they are related by the same condensation process, differing only in freezing.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author make in this passage?
A) Frost is to dew as snow is to hail
B) Frost is to dew as snow is to rain
C) Dew is to frost as rain is to snow
D) Frost is to snow as dew is to rain

Key Takeaway
Frost, dew, snow, and rain all stem from the same process of vapor condensation, differing only in temperature and obstacles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you now pay attention to the details of creation that you previously ignored?
?
The passage describes how obstacles prevent vapor from rising to its intended height. What obstacles in your life have prevented you from rising to your spiritual potential? Are you going to remove those obstacles or use them as excuses to stay grounded?

Thunder, Lightning, and Thunderbolt
Explains physical causes of thunder, lightning, and thunderbolts from smoke in clouds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how medieval Islamic philosophy explains thunder, lightning, and thunderbolts as resulting from smoke trapped in clouds.

Core Idea
Thunder, lightning, and thunderbolts are caused by smoke tearing through clouds.

Having explained rain clouds, the text now introduces smoke as a key element. As vapor rises, it carries smoke with it. When trapped in a cloud, this smoke tears the cloud violently, producing sound and fire. This passage draws on Aristotelian meteorology to explain these dramatic phenomena in purely physical terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the role of smoke.
2
Understand the role of smoke
Smoke rises with vapor, becomes trapped in the cloud, and either rises or descends based on its temperature.
3
Identify the three phenomena
Notice that the tearing and friction produce three things: thunder (sound), lightning (fine fire), and thunderbolt (dense fire).
These are distinct outcomes of the same process.

Source Text

Meteors, Comets, and Sky Fires
Explains physical causes of meteors, comets, and descending fire from smoke ignition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author explains meteors, comets, and other sky fires as thick smoke igniting or burning in the celestial sphere.

Core Idea
Meteors and comets are thick smoke that ignites or undergoes sustained combustion near the sphere of fire.

The final part of this passage deals with more spectacular phenomena: shooting stars, comets with tails, and fire descending from the sky. The author uses the analogy of a lamp re-lighting to explain how smoke ignites upon reaching the sphere of fire. He then describes how smoke can burn continuously, forming various shapes, and how a continuous smoke trail can appear as a dragon descending.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the different phenomena described.
2
Understand the meteor (shihab)
Notice the analogy: thick smoke ignites upon reaching the sphere of fire, like a snuffed lamp re-lighting from a flame.
3
Distinguish comets
If the smoke is too thick to ignite entirely, it burns slowly and takes shapes like tails or animals.
4
Learn about descending fire
If the smoke's connection to earth is continuous and it ignites downward, it appears as a dragon descending.
This is called 'al-hariq'.

If it freezes, it descends as frost; otherwise as dew, so the ratio of frost to dew is the ratio of snow to rain. And the rain cloud may be from abundant vapor that condenses due to cold without ascending to the frigid zone, due to an obstacle such as winds that prevent vapors from rising, or that compress them to gather, because of mountains standing before the winds, and such as the heaviness of the preceding part and the slowness of its motion. And the thick smoke may ignite upon reaching the sphere of fire, as is observed when the smoke of a snuffed-out lamp reaches a burning lamp and the flame spreads into it, so it appears as if a falling star has fallen—that is the meteor. And the smoke, due to its thickness, may not ignite, but rather burns and combustion persists in it, so it remains in the form of a tail or a mane or a snake or an animal with horns, and perhaps it stops beneath a star, and rotates with the fire by the rotation of the celestial sphere, and perhaps there appear in it terrifying signs, red and black, according to the increase in the thickness of the smoke. And if the connection of the smoke from the earth is not cut off, and its ignition descends to the earth, it appears as if a dragon is descending from the sky to the earth—that is the fire.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage provides a natural philosophical explanation for various atmospheric and celestial phenomena. It begins by explaining the formation of frost and dew, analogizing the ratio of frost to dew as that of snow to rain. It then describes how rain clouds can form from abundant vapor that condenses due to cold without reaching the frigid zone, due to obstacles like winds or mountains. The text then introduces the role of smoke accompanying rising vapor. When both vapor and smoke rise into cold air, the vapor condenses into a cloud and the smoke is trapped. If the smoke retains heat it rises, if cooled it descends, and in either case it violently tears the cloud, producing thunder from tearing and friction, lightning as a fine fiery substance, and the thunderbolt as a dense one. Next, it explains that thick smoke may ignite upon reaching the sphere of fire, analogous to a snuffed-out lamp wick relighting from a burning flame, and this appears as a falling star or meteor. If the smoke is too thick to ignite entirely, it undergoes sustained combustion, taking forms like a tail, mane, snake, or horned animal, and may appear to rotate with a star or produce red and black signs depending on smoke density. Finally, if the smoke's connection to the earth is continuous and its ignition descends, it appears as a descending dragon—that is, a fire from the sky.

Key Terms
شهاب
كرة النار
فلك
حريق
دخان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Shooting stars are stars falling from the sky or purely supernatural signs.
Correction: This passage explains them as thick smoke igniting in the sphere of fire, a natural physical process.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to explain the meteor (shihab)?
A) A lamp being lit by the sun
B) The smoke of a snuffed-out lamp re-lighting from a burning lamp
C) A fire spreading through a forest
D) A star moving through the sky

Key Takeaway
Meteors, comets, and sky fires are explained as thick smoke that either ignites or burns slowly in the celestial sphere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Commit tonight to looking up and saying, 'Subhanallah, what a precise system You have made.'

What does your reaction reveal about your reliance on Allah's created order? This week, when you see a strange sky, pause to reflect on its cause before fearing it—and turn your fear into tawheed.

Formation of Winds from Condensed Smoke
Introduces the condensation of smoke as the cause of winds and mentions their complexity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Islamic cosmology explains wind formation through the condensation of smoke and its interaction with celestial spheres and temperature layers.

Core Idea
Winds arise from the condensation of smoke and its interaction with celestial motions.

In this passage, the author builds on a quotation from al-Mawaqif to explain the natural causes of winds. He first presents the standard view that smoke condenses due to cold and descends, causing air to wave. He then offers his own detailed explanation distinguishing cold and hot winds, and clarifies a technical point about smoke's contact with the celestial sphere.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the two distinct mechanisms for cold and hot wind.
The passage begins with a quotation from al-Mawaqif (marked by 'He said').
2
Identify the two wind types
Notice that cold wind occurs when smoke's heat is broken by the frigid layer (zamhaririyya), and hot wind occurs when smoke reaches the sphere of fire and returns with celestial motion.
3
Understand the clarification about collision
The author explains that 'colliding with the celestial sphere' means adjacency, not literal impact, because smoke cannot penetrate the sphere of fire.
This resolves a potential misconception.
4
Consider the alternative cause
The passage ends by noting that wind can also result from rarefaction of air, not only from smoke condensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وقد تتكاثف الأدخنة) المتصاعدة بالبرد فتنزل بطبعها، وبرد حركة الفلك إياها عند وصولها إلى كرة النار، فيتموج الهواء وهو الريح، وقد يلتقي ريحان من جهتين، فيستديران فتحدث الزوابع وأما في الرياح من اختلاف الأحوال، واشتداد الأهوال، بحيث يقلع الأشجار، ويختطف السفن من البحار، مع أن رجوع الدخان ينبغي أن يكون على استقامة كالبخار، فلم يتكلموا فيه بشيء يعول عليه، ولم يتشرفوا من مرسل الرياح بنفحات الرجوع. إشارة إلى أسباب الرياح، وذلك أن الأدخنة الكثيرة المتصاعدة قد تتكاثف بالبرد وينكسر حرها بالطبقة الزمهريرية، فتثقل وترجع بطبعها، فيتموج الهواء، فتحدث الريح الباردة وقد لا ينكسر حرها، فتتصاعد إلى كرة النار، ثم ترجع بحركتها التابعة بحركة الفلك، فتحدث الريح الحارة، وعلى هذا ينبغي أن يحمل وقع في المواقف من أنها تصادم الفلك أي تقارنه، بحيث يصل إليهـا أثـر حركته، وإلا فلا يتصور أن يقطع الدخان مع ما فيه من الأجزاء الأرضية الثقيلة كرة مع شدّة إحالتها لما يجاورها حتى يصادم الفلك حقيقة، وقد يكون تموج الهواء لتخلخل يقع في جانب منه، فيدفع ما يجاوره، وهكذا إلى أن يفترق بالجملة، فالتموج من الهواء هو الريح بأي سبب يقع.
He said: "And the rising smoke may thicken due to cold and descend by its nature, and the cold of the celestial sphere's motion [affects] them when they reach the sphere of fire, so the air waves—that is the wind. And two winds may meet from two directions and rotate, causing whirlwinds. As for winds, [there are] variations in conditions and intensity of terrors, such that they uproot trees and snatch ships from the seas, even though the return of smoke should be straight like vapor; so they did not speak about it with anything reliable, and they did not receive from the sender of the winds through the breaths of return." An indication of the causes of winds: that the abundant rising smoke may thicken due to cold and its heat is broken by the frigid layer, so it becomes heavy and returns by its nature, thus the air waves, producing cold wind. And its heat may not be broken, so it rises to the sphere of fire, then returns with its motion following the motion of the celestial sphere, producing hot wind. And on this should be understood what occurs in al-Mawaqif that they collide with the celestial sphere, i.e., they are adjacent to it, so that the effect of its motion reaches them; otherwise, it is inconceivable that smoke, containing heavy earthy parts, cuts through a sphere [of fire] despite its intense alteration of what surrounds it, so that it actually collides with the celestial sphere. And the air may wave due to rarefaction occurring on one side, pushing what is adjacent, and so on until it separates entirely. Thus the waving of the air is the wind, regardless of the cause.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage begins with a quotation from the original text (al-Mawaqif) stating that rising smoke condenses due to cold and descends, and the cold motion of the celestial sphere affects it when it reaches the sphere of fire, causing air to wave—that is wind. It notes that two winds meeting from opposite directions can produce whirlwinds. The commentator then observes that despite the terrible effects of winds (uprooting trees, snatching ships), the process of smoke returning should be straight like vapor, implying that existing explanations are insufficient. He then provides his own indication: abundant rising smoke may thicken due to cold, its heat broken by the frigid layer (al-zamhaririyya), making it heavy and descend, causing cold wind. Alternatively, if its heat is not broken, it rises to the sphere of fire and then returns following the motion of the celestial sphere, causing hot wind. He clarifies a statement in al-Mawaqif about smoke colliding with the celestial sphere as meaning 'adjacent to' or 'reaching the effect of its motion', not actual collision, since smoke cannot cut through the sphere of fire. Finally, he notes that air waving may also result from rarefaction on one side pushing adjacent air, and that any such waving is called wind.

زمهريرية

تموج
زوبعة
إعصار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that wind is caused by smoke physically colliding with the celestial sphere.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'collision' means adjacency; smoke only reaches near the sphere to be affected by its motion, not actual impact.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what produces hot wind?
A) Smoke that is broken by the frigid layer
B) Smoke that rises to the sphere of fire and returns with the celestial motion
C) Two winds meeting from opposite directions
D) Rarefaction on one side of the air

Key Takeaway
Wind originates from condensed smoke that either descends due to cold (cold wind) or rises to the celestial sphere (hot wind), and this explanation resolves problems with earlier accounts.

Causes of Whirlwinds: Ascending and Descending
Explains ascending and descending whirlwinds and limits of their explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proposed natural causes of ascending and descending whirlwinds as explained in the text, and recognize the author's critical stance on the limitations of these explanations.

Core Idea
Whirlwinds arise from colliding winds, but their true cause remains uncertain.

This lesson delves into the causes of two types of whirlwinds: ascending (زوبعة) and descending (إعصار). The author presents specific mechanisms based on wind collisions and cloud interactions, but ultimately acknowledges that these explanations are not fully reliable. As you read, pay attention to the two distinct causal scenarios and the concluding note of epistemic humility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the literal English translation. Notice the technical terms for whirlwinds and wind material.
The passage uses compact classical Arabic; take your time.
2
Identify the two types of whirlwinds
The passage distinguishes between ascending (زوبعة) and descending (إعصار) whirlwinds. Find the cause given for each type.
The ascending has two possible causes; the descending has one.
3
Understand the mechanical causes
For the ascending, note the striking of wind on earth and meeting of opposite winds. For the descending, note the interaction between a descending wind and an ascending cloud.
4
Reflect on the author's critique
The author ends by stating that the observed violent winds (uprooting trees, snatching ships) have no reliable explanation, despite the return of smoke being straight like vapor. This shows scholarly honesty about uncertainty.
Consider why the author mentions smoke – it is a contrast to the straight behavior of vapor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الزوبعة والإعصار، أعني الريح المستديرة الصاعدة أو الهابطة، فسبب الصاعدة أن المادة الريحية إذا وصلت إلى الأرض وقرعتها عنيفاً ثم رجعت فلقيتها ريح أخرى من جهتها وكونها حدثت الحالة المذكورة وقد تحدث من تلاقي الريحين من جهتين متقابلتين، وسبب الهابطة أن ينفصل ريح من سحابة، فيقصد النزول فيعارضها في الطريق سحابة صاعدة، فتدافعها الأجزاء الريحية إلى تحت فيقع جزء من الريح بين دافع إلى تحت ودافع إلى فوق، فيستدير وتنضغط الأجزاء الأرضية بينها، فتهبط ملتوية، والحق أن ما شوهد من أحوال الرياح القالعة للأشجار، والمختطفة للسفن من البحار، مع أن رجوع الدخان ينبغي أن يكون على استقامة كالبخار، لم يتكلموا فيه بشيء يعول عليه، ولم يتشرفوا من مرسل الرياح بنفحات الرجوع.
As for the whirlwind and the cyclone—I mean the circular rising or descending wind—the cause of the rising one is that when the wind material reaches the earth and strikes it violently, then returns, another wind from its direction meets it, and that causes the aforementioned state. And it may also occur from the meeting of two winds from opposite directions. The cause of the descending one is that a wind separates from a cloud and intends to descend, but a rising cloud opposes it on the way, so the wind particles push it downward, and a part of the wind falls between a downward push and an upward push, so it rotates, and the earthy parts are compressed between them, so it descends twisted. The truth is that what is observed of the states of winds that uproot trees and snatch ships from the seas, even though the return of smoke should be straight like vapor, they have not spoken about it with anything reliable, and they have not received from the sender of the winds through the breaths of return.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage specifically addresses two types of whirlwinds: the ascending (al-zawba'a) and the descending (al-i'sar). For the ascending one, two causes are given: either the wind material strikes the earth violently and rebounds, meeting another wind from the same direction; or two winds from opposite directions meet. For the descending one, a wind separates from a cloud and attempts to descend, but encounters a rising cloud; the wind particles push it downward, and a part of the wind ends up between two opposing forces (upward and downward), causing it to rotate, compress earthy parts, and descend in a twisted manner. The passage concludes with a critical remark: despite observed violent winds, the existing explanations are not reliable, and the true cause remains unknown—a gesture of epistemic humility.

Misconception: Whirlwinds are caused by jinn or supernatural forces.
Correction: The text explains whirlwinds as natural phenomena arising from collisions of wind currents and cloud interactions, though it admits that the exact causes of violent winds are uncertain.

Critique of Explanations for Violent Winds
Casts doubt on received explanations for violent winds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the author critiques existing explanations for violent winds, emphasizing the limits of human reason in explaining certain natural phenomena.

Core Idea
Not all natural phenomena can be explained by human reason alone.

This short passage serves as a critical concluding remark. The author points out a discrepancy between observed violent winds and theoretical expectations, highlighting that some natural events defy easy explanation. It underscores the need for epistemic humility when confronting natural causality.

Guided Path
1

This indicates epistemic caution and an acknowledgment of the limits of human reason in explaining certain natural phenomena.

The Unexplained Power of Winds
Initial critique of material explanations for winds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that material explanations fail to account for the observed power and diversity of winds.

Core Idea
Winds' violent behavior defies simple material causation.

The author begins by drawing attention to the terrifying and destructive power of winds—uprooting trees, snatching ships. He points out that if winds were merely smoky vapors rising and falling, they should move straight and gently, like rising smoke. The observed violence, then, already challenges any purely material explanation. This lesson focuses on that initial critique, which sets the stage for a deeper theological argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening critique
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how the author contrasts the expected behavior of smoke with the actual destructive force of winds.
The phrase 'نفحات الرجوع' (breaths of return) is a subtle reference to divine sending.
2
Identify the key observation
The author notes that smoke should rise and fall straight like vapor, but winds do not. This is a problem for materialist theories.
3
Reflect on the rhetorical force
Consider why the author begins with the failure of material explanations before presenting them. This sets up the need for a higher cause.
The phrase 'لم يتكلموا فيه بشيء يعول عليه' (they have not spoken anything reliable) is a strong dismissal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الرياح من اختلاف الأحوال، واشتداد الأهوال، بحيث يقلع الأشجار، ويختطف السفن من البحار ، مع أن رجوع الدخان ينبغي أن يكون على استقامة ( ) كالبخار ، فلم يتكلموا فيه بشيء يعول عليه، ولم يتشرفوا من مرسل الرياح بنفحات الرجوع ] . إشارة إلى أسباب الرياح، وذلك أن الأدخنة الكثيرة المتصاعدة قد تتكاثف بالبرد وينكسر حرها بالطبقة الزمهريرية، فتثقل وترجع بطبعها، فيتموج الهواء، فتحدث الريح الباردة وقد لا ينكسر حرها، فتتصاعد إلى كرة النار، ثم ترجع بحركتها التابعة بحركة الفلك، فتحدث الريح الحارة، وعلى هذا ينبغي أن يحمل وقع في المواقف من أنها تصادم .الفلك أي تقارنه، بحيث يصل إليهـا أثـر حركته، وإلا فلا يتصور أن يقطع الدخان مع ما فيه من الأجزاء الأرضية الثقيلة كرة مع شدة إحالتها لما يجاورها حتى يصادم الفلك حقيقة، وقد يكون تموج الهواء لتخلخل يقع في جانب منه ، فيدفع ما يجاوره، وهكذا إلى أن يفترق بالجملة، فالتموج من الهواء هو الريح بأي سبب يقع، وأما الزوبعة والإعصار، أعني الريح المستديرة الصاعدة أو الهابطة ، فسبب الصاعدة (أن المادة الريحية إذا وصلت إلى الأرض وقرعتها عنيفاً ثم رجعت فلقيتها ريح أخرى من جهتها وكونها حدثت الحالة المذكورة وقد تحدث من تلاقي الريحين من جهتين متقابلتين، وسبب الهابطة أن ينفصل ريح من سحابة ، فيقصد النزول فيعارضها في الطريق سحابة صاعدة ، فتدافعها الأجزاء الريحية إلى تحت فيقع جزء من الريح بين دافع إلى تحت ودافع إلى فوق، فيستدير وتنضغط الأجزاء الأرضية بينها، فتهبط ملتوية، والحق أن ما شوهد من أحوال الرياح القالعة للأشجار، والمختطفة للسفن من البحار، وما تواتر من تخريبها للمدن وما ورد من النصوص القاطعة ذلك يشهد شهادة صادقة بوجوب الرجوع إلى القادر المختار سبحانه وتعالى وغاية ما ذكروه لو ثبت بيان الأسباب المادية
As for the winds, from diversity of states and severity of terrors, such that they uproot trees and snatch ships from the seas, whereas the return of smoke should be straight like vapor, they have not spoken about it with anything reliable, nor have they honored from the sender of winds the breaths of return. An indication to the causes of winds: that many ascending smokes may become thick by cold and their heat broken by the frigid layer, so they become heavy and return by their nature, then the air becomes wavy, and cold wind occurs. And sometimes their heat is not broken, so they ascend to the sphere of fire, then return by their motion following the motion of the celestial sphere, so hot wind occurs. And upon this should be carried what occurred in al-Mawaqif that they collide with the celestial sphere, i.e., they approach it such that the effect of its motion reaches them. Otherwise, it is not conceivable that smoke, despite its heavy earthly parts, cuts through the sphere given its intense transformation of what adjoins it until it truly collides with the celestial sphere. And sometimes the waving of air is due to rarefaction occurring in one side, pushing what adjoins it, and so on until it disperses entirely. Thus, the waving of air is wind by whatever cause it occurs. As for the whirlwind and cyclone, meaning the circular wind ascending or descending: the cause of the ascending one is that when the wind material reaches the ground and strikes it violently, then returns, it meets another wind from its side and both cause the mentioned state, and it may occur from the meeting of two winds from two opposite sides. The cause of the descending one is that a wind separates from a cloud and intends to descend, but an ascending cloud opposes it on the way, so the wind particles push it downward, and a part of the wind falls between a downward push and an upward push, so it turns and the earthly particles are compressed among them, so it descends twisted. The truth is that what is observed of the conditions of winds that uproot trees and snatch ships from the seas, and what is recurrent of their destruction of cities, and what has come of definitive texts, all bear truthful witness to the necessity of returning to the Omnipotent Free Agent, exalted is He. And the utmost of what they mentioned, if established, is a statement of material causes.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of purely material explanations for winds and hurricanes. The author begins by noting the extreme power of winds—uprooting trees, snatching ships—and points out that if smoke simply rose and fell naturally, it should do so straight like vapor, yet winds behave violently. He then presents the physicists' explanation: cold smoke becomes heavy and falls, causing cold winds; hot smoke rises to the fiery sphere and returns with celestial motion, causing hot winds. He acknowledges that this is the basis of the theory found in al-Mawaqif. However, he objects that it is inconceivable for heavy earthly smoke to truly reach the celestial sphere. He also describes mechanical causes for whirlwinds and cyclones, both ascending and descending. Finally, he concludes that the observed destructive power of winds, along with definitive religious texts, proves the necessity of the Omnipotent Free Agent. The materialist explanations, even if true, only describe secondary causes and do not eliminate the need for a divine power.

Key Terms
الريح
السبب المادي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Winds can be fully explained by the natural rising and falling of smoke or vapors.
Correction: The author argues that the violent and varied behavior of winds (uprooting trees, snatching ships) is not consistent with the simple, straight motion of smoke, indicating the insufficiency of such material explanations.

Checkpoint

A) It is well-established and reliable.
B) It is not reliable and fails to account for the observed power of winds.
C) It is the only possible explanation.
D) It is correct but incomplete.

Key Takeaway
The observed destructive power of winds cannot be explained by simple material causes, prompting the need for a divine explanation.

?

From Material Causes to the Omnipotent
Presentation and critique of mechanistic wind theories, concluding with the Omnipotent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author presents and critiques mechanistic explanations of winds and whirlwinds, concluding that only the Omnipotent Free Agent can account for their power.

Core Idea
Even if material causes exist, they point to a higher Agent.

The author now turns to the actual material theories of winds proposed by physicists. He explains their accounts of hot and cold winds, the role of the celestial sphere, and even a mechanical description of whirlwinds. However, he raises a fundamental objection: it is impossible for heavy earthly smoke to truly reach the celestial sphere. Finally, he concludes that the observed effects of winds—combined with revealed texts—necessitate a return to the Omnipotent Free Agent. This lesson follows that argument from start to finish.

Guided Path
1
Understand the material theories
Read the descriptions of cold wind (due to cold, heavy smoke returning) and hot wind (due to smoke reaching the fiery sphere and returning with celestial motion). Notice the reference to al-Mawaqif.
The author is engaging with the natural philosophy of his time.
2
Identify the objection
Find where the author objects that smoke cannot actually collide with the celestial sphere because of its heavy earthly parts and the sphere's transformative nature.
This is a physical impossibility argument.
3
Examine the explanation of whirlwinds
Read how ascending and descending whirlwinds are mechanically described. Note the interplay of opposing winds and the formation of circular motion.
The author presents these as part of the materialist account.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
The author states that observed destructive power and definitive divine texts point decisively to the Omnipotent Free Agent, not just material causes.
The phrase 'غاية ما ذكروه لو ثبت بيان الأسباب المادية' means that even if the material causes were proven, they are only secondary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الرياح من اختلاف الأحوال، واشتداد الأهوال، بحيث يقلع الأشجار، ويختطف السفن من البحار ، مع أن رجوع الدخان ينبغي أن يكون على استقامة ( ) كالبخار ، فلم يتكلموا فيه بشيء يعول عليه، ولم يتشرفوا من مرسل الرياح بنفحات الرجوع ] . إشارة إلى أسباب الرياح، وذلك أن الأدخنة الكثيرة المتصاعدة قد تتكاثف بالبرد وينكسر حرها بالطبقة الزمهريرية، فتثقل وترجع بطبعها، فيتموج الهواء، فتحدث الريح الباردة وقد لا ينكسر حرها، فتتصاعد إلى كرة النار، ثم ترجع بحركتها التابعة بحركة الفلك، فتحدث الريح الحارة، وعلى هذا ينبغي أن يحمل وقع في المواقف من أنها تصادم .الفلك أي تقارنه، بحيث يصل إليهـا أثـر حركته، وإلا فلا يتصور أن يقطع الدخان مع ما فيه من الأجزاء الأرضية الثقيلة كرة مع شدة إحالتها لما يجاورها حتى يصادم الفلك حقيقة، وقد يكون تموج الهواء لتخلخل يقع في جانب منه ، فيدفع ما يجاوره، وهكذا إلى أن يفترق بالجملة، فالتموج من الهواء هو الريح بأي سبب يقع، وأما الزوبعة والإعصار، أعني الريح المستديرة الصاعدة أو الهابطة ، فسبب الصاعدة (أن المادة الريحية إذا وصلت إلى الأرض وقرعتها عنيفاً ثم رجعت فلقيتها ريح أخرى من جهتها وكونها حدثت الحالة المذكورة وقد تحدث من تلاقي الريحين من جهتين متقابلتين، وسبب الهابطة أن ينفصل ريح من سحابة ، فيقصد النزول فيعارضها في الطريق سحابة صاعدة ، فتدافعها الأجزاء الريحية إلى تحت فيقع جزء من الريح بين دافع إلى تحت ودافع إلى فوق، فيستدير وتنضغط الأجزاء الأرضية بينها، فتهبط ملتوية، والحق أن ما شوهد من أحوال الرياح القالعة للأشجار، والمختطفة للسفن من البحار، وما تواتر من تخريبها للمدن وما ورد من النصوص القاطعة ذلك يشهد شهادة صادقة بوجوب الرجوع إلى القادر المختار سبحانه وتعالى وغاية ما ذكروه لو ثبت بيان الأسباب المادية
As for the winds, from diversity of states and severity of terrors, such that they uproot trees and snatch ships from the seas, whereas the return of smoke should be straight like vapor, they have not spoken about it with anything reliable, nor have they honored from the sender of winds the breaths of return. An indication to the causes of winds: that many ascending smokes may become thick by cold and their heat broken by the frigid layer, so they become heavy and return by their nature, then the air becomes wavy, and cold wind occurs. And sometimes their heat is not broken, so they ascend to the sphere of fire, then return by their motion following the motion of the celestial sphere, so hot wind occurs. And upon this should be carried what occurred in al-Mawaqif that they collide with the celestial sphere, i.e., they approach it such that the effect of its motion reaches them. Otherwise, it is not conceivable that smoke, despite its heavy earthly parts, cuts through the sphere given its intense transformation of what adjoins it until it truly collides with the celestial sphere. And sometimes the waving of air is due to rarefaction occurring in one side, pushing what adjoins it, and so on until it disperses entirely. Thus, the waving of air is wind by whatever cause it occurs. As for the whirlwind and cyclone, meaning the circular wind ascending or descending: the cause of the ascending one is that when the wind material reaches the ground and strikes it violently, then returns, it meets another wind from its side and both cause the mentioned state, and it may occur from the meeting of two winds from two opposite sides. The truth is that what is observed of the conditions of winds that uproot trees and snatch ships from the seas, and what is recurrent of their destruction of cities, and what has come of definitive texts, all bear truthful witness to the necessity of returning to the Omnipotent Free Agent, exalted is He. And the utmost of what they mentioned, if established, is a statement of material causes.

Explanation of Halo and Rainbow
Quotes and explains the causes of halo and rainbow.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the medieval Islamic physical explanation for the halo and rainbow phenomena based on reflection from fine polished particles.

Core Idea
Halo and rainbow arise from reflected light off fine polished cloud particles.

This passage from a classical theological work presents a detailed physical explanation of the halo (around the moon and sun) and the rainbow. It describes how light from the eye reflects off fine polished particles in a moist cloud, creating these circular and colored phenomena. The author then provides a commentary explaining the mechanism of reflection, emphasizing that the moon itself is seen directly, while small mirrors only transmit light, not shape.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the technical terms for the phenomena.
The passage includes both the original quote and the commentator's explanation.
2
Identify the two phenomena
Notice that the passage describes two distinct phenomena: the halo (around the moon and sun) and the rainbow. Understand how each is formed according to the text.
3
Understand the reflection mechanism
Focus on the explanation of the halo: fine polished particles in a cloud act like mirrors. Light from the eye reflects off these particles to the moon, and then back, creating a white circle. The rainbow involves a similar reflection but in a different orientation.
The commentator clarifies that the moon itself is seen directly, while each particle shows an image due to reflection.
4
Note the distinction between object and image
The passage explains that when the particles are too small, they only transmit light and not the shape, so a circle is seen rather than a clear image. This is an important optical point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ( وقد يحول) إليه بين القمر والبصر غيم رطب رقيق أبيض متصغر الأجزاء، تتفق أجزاء أوضاعها، فينعكس ضوء البصر من أجزاء ذلك الغيم إلى جرم القمر، فيؤدي كل جزء ضوء القمر، فيرى دائرة بيضاء تامة أو ناقصة، وتسمى الهالة (۳). وقد تتضاعف وقد يحدث مثلها للشمس وتسمى الطغاوة، وإذا وقع مثل تلك الأجزاء في خلاف جهة الشمس حصل فيه من انعكاس شعاع البصر ،قوس، جانبها الأعلى أحمر ناصع، والأسفل أرجواني، والأوسط كرائي ويسمى قوس قزح . يشير إلى سبب الهالة وقوس قزح. أما الهالة فسببها إحاطة أجزاء رشية صقيلة كأنها مرايا متراصة بغيم رقيق لطيف لا يستر ما وراءه واقع في مقابلة القمر، فيرى في ذلك الغيم نفس القمر، لأن الشيء إنما يرى على الاستقامة نفسه لا شبحه، ويرى في كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء الرشية شبحه لانعكاس ضوء البصر منها إلى القمر، لأن الضوء إذا وقع على صقيل انعكس إلى الجسم الذي وضعه من ذلك الصقيل كوضع المضيء منه إذا لم تكن جهته مخالفة لجهة المضيء، فيرى ضوء القمر شكله (۹) ، لأن المرأة إذا كانت صغيرة لا تؤدي شكل المرئي، بل ضوءه ولا يرى
He said: 'And sometimes there comes between the moon and the sight a moist, fine, white cloud with small parts, the parts of its positions coincide, so the light of sight is reflected from the parts of that cloud to the moon's body, so each part conveys the moon's light, so a complete or incomplete white circle is seen, called the halo. It may be doubled, and a similar thing may occur for the sun, called the sun-halo. And if such parts occur in the opposite direction of the sun, there results from the reflection of the light of sight a bow, its upper side bright red, the lower violet, and the middle bluish, called the rainbow.' He points to the cause of the halo and rainbow. As for the halo, its cause is the surrounding of fine, polished particles, like arranged mirrors, by a fine, delicate cloud that does not veil what is behind it, situated opposite the moon. So in that cloud the moon itself is seen, because a thing is seen directly itself, not its image. And in each of those fine particles its image is seen due to the reflection of the light of sight from them to the moon, because when light falls on a polished surface, it is reflected to the body that is placed from that polished surface like the position of the luminous one from it if its direction is not opposite to the direction of the luminous. So the light of the moon is seen, but not its shape, because if the mirror is small, it does not convey the shape of the seen object, but only its light, and it is not seen.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage quotes and explains the physical causes of the halo and rainbow. The author first presents the original text: between the moon and the eye a fine, moist cloud appears; the parts of the cloud reflect light from the eye to the moon, so each part conveys the moon's light, forming a white circle called the halo. A similar phenomenon occurs around the sun, called the sun-halo. When such parts are opposite the sun, a bow with colored bands appears, called the rainbow. The commentator then explains the mechanism: for the halo, polished particles surrounding a fine cloud act like mirrors, reflecting the moon's light directly, so the moon itself is seen in the cloud. However, since the particles are small, they only transmit the light, not the shape, so a circle is seen instead of a clear image. The rainbow is not further detailed here, but the explanation implies a similar reflection process.

الطغاوة
قوس قزح
انعكاس
صقيل
غيم رطب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The physical explanation of these phenomena contradicts divine causation.
Correction: The passage merely describes the natural order placed by God; it does not deny divine causation but explains the secondary causes.

Checkpoint

A) Refraction of light through ice crystals.
B) Reflection of light from polished particles in a fine cloud opposite the moon.
C) Direct emission of light from the moon interacting with air.

Key Takeaway
The halo and rainbow are explained as natural phenomena caused by reflection of light from fine polished particles in clouds, a view that integrates physical causality within a theological framework.

Defining the Halo and Rainbow Phenomena
Author enumerates three optical phenomena: moon halo, sun halo, rainbow.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the moon's halo, the sun's halo, and the rainbow as described in the text.

Core Idea
Halos and rainbows are optical phenomena from moisture particles.

The author opens with a concise description of three common atmospheric phenomena. This lesson presents them as a classification before diving into their causes. Reading this carefully will help you identify each phenomenon later.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text from 'قال (وقد يحول)' to 'ويسمى قوس قزح' once. Notice the three phenomena mentioned.
The passage is dense; focus on the names: الهالة, الطغاوة, قوس قزح.
2
Identify each phenomenon
List the three phenomena: the moon's halo, the sun's halo, and the rainbow. Note any distinctive features mentioned (color, completeness).
The rainbow has specific colors: red, violet, carmine.
3
Understand the common cause
Notice that all three involve 'أجزاء رشية' (dew-like parts) and reflection. This sets up the detailed explanation later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وقد يحول) إليه بين القمر والبصر غيم رطب رقيق أبيض متصغر الأجزاء، تتفق أجزاء أوضاعها، فينعكس ضوء البصر من أجزاء ذلك الغيم إلى جرم القمر، فيؤدي كل جزء ضوء القمر، فيرى دائرة بيضاء تامة أو ناقصة، وتسمى الهالة. وقد تتضاعف وقد يحدث مثلها للشمس وتسمى الطغاوة، وإذا وقع مثل تلك الأجزاء في خلاف جهة الشمس حصل فيه من انعكاس شعاع البصر قوس، جانبها الأعلى أحمر ناصع، والأسفل أرجواني، والأوسط كرائي ويسمى قوس قزح. يشير إلى سبب الهالة وقوس قزح. أما الهالة فسببها إحاطة أجزاء رشية صقيلة كأنها مرايا متراصة بغيم رقيق لطيف لا يستر ما وراءه واقع في مقابلة القمر، فيرى في ذلك الغيم نفس القمر، لأن الشيء إنما يرى على الاستقامة نفسه لا شبحه، ويرى في كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء الرشية شبحه لانعكاس ضوء البصر منها إلى القمر، لأن الضوء إذا وقع على صقيل انعكس إلى الجسم الذي وضعه من ذلك الصقيل كوضع المضيء منه إذا لم تكن جهته مخالفة لجهة المضيء، فيرى ضوء القمر شكله، لأن المرآة إذا كانت صغيرة لا تؤدي شكل المرئي، بل ضوءه ولا يرى لونه إن كان ملوناً، فيؤدي كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء ضوء القمر، فيرى دائرة مضيئة لكون الهيئة الحاصلة بين تلك الأجزاء، وبين المرئي واحدة، وإنما لا يرى السحاب الذي يقابل القمر لقوة شعاع القمر، فإن الرقيق اللطيف لا يرى في ضوء القوي كأجزاء الهباء المتفرقة في الصحراء، وأكثر ما تحدث الهالة عند عدم الريح، فيستدل بتخرقها من جميع الجهات على الصحو، ومن جهة على ريح تأتي من تلك الجهة، وببطلانها يثخن السحاب على المطر لتكثر الأجزاء المائية، وقد تتضاعف الهالة، بأن توجد سحابتان بالصفة المذكورة، إحداهما تحت الأخرى، ولا محالة تكون التحتانية أعظم لكونها أقرب. وذكر بعضهم أنه رأى هالات معاً، وأما سبع هالة الشمس وتسمى بالطغاوة فنادرة جداً.
He said (and it may change) between the moon and the sight a moist, thin, white cloud with small parts, their positions coinciding, then the light of sight reflects from the parts of that cloud to the body of the moon, so each part conveys the light of the moon, and a complete or incomplete white circle is seen, and it is called the halo. And it may be multiplied, and a similar thing may occur for the sun and is called the الطغاوة (sun halo). And if such parts occur opposite the direction of the sun, there occurs from the reflection of the ray of sight an arc, its upper side bright red, the lower violet, and the middle carmine, and it is called the rainbow. He points to the cause of the halo and the rainbow. As for the halo, its cause is the surrounding of polished dew-like parts, as if they are compact mirrors, by a thin, delicate cloud that does not conceal what is behind it, placed opposite the moon, then in that cloud the moon itself is seen, because a thing is seen directly itself, not its image, and in each one of those dew-like parts its image is seen because of the reflection of the light of sight from them to the moon, for when light falls on a polished surface, it reflects to the body that is positioned from that polished surface as the position of the luminous from it if its direction is not opposite the direction of the luminous, so the light of the moon and its shape are seen, because the mirror, if it is small, does not convey the shape of the seen object, but its light, and its color is not seen if it is colored, so each one of those parts conveys the light of the moon, and a luminous circle is seen because the configuration between those parts and the seen object is one. And the cloud that faces the moon is not seen because of the strength of the moon's ray, for the thin delicate is not seen in strong light, like the scattered dust particles in the desert. And most often the halo occurs when there is no wind, so it is inferred from its tearing from all directions to clear weather, and from one direction to wind coming from that direction, and by its invalidation, the cloud thickens to rain due to the abundance of watery parts. And the halo may be multiplied by the existence of two clouds with the described quality, one beneath the other, and inevitably the lower is larger because it is nearer. And some mentioned that they saw halos together. As for the sun's halo, called الطغاوة, it is very rare.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage defines the halo (الهالة) and explains its physical cause. The author first describes the observable phenomenon: a white circle around the moon caused by a thin cloud with fine particles that reflect moonlight. He also mentions the sun's halo (الطغاوة) and gives a brief note on the rainbow (قوس قزح), which will be treated separately. The core explanation uses the principle of reflection: polished dew-like parts act like small mirrors; each reflects the moon's light to the eye, producing a luminous circle. The author clarifies that the cloud itself is not seen because the moon's light is stronger, similar to dust particles invisible in sunlight. Practical signs are also given: the halo's behavior can predict weather changes (clear skies or rain). The possibility of multiple halos is explained by stacked clouds, and the sun's halo is noted as very rare. The passage follows a pattern of definition, causal explanation, and application.

Key Terms
هالة
طغاوة
قوس قزح
أجزاء رشية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the name of the sun's halo?
A) الهالة
B) الطغاوة
C) قوس قزح
D) الأجزاء الرشية

Key Takeaway
The moon halo, sun halo, and rainbow are all optical phenomena involving reflection from small moisture particles in the sky.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a rainbow or a halo, do you immediately recognize it as a sign of Allah’s precise order, or do you simply admire it without lifting your heart to the Creator? What would it take for you to turn every such sight into a moment of gratitude?
?
The author describes these phenomena as caused by simple physical laws—moisture and reflection. Have you ever let the beauty of nature distract you from remembering that every detail is under Allah's command? From now on, when you witness a natural wonder, will you pause and say, 'SubhanAllah,' and reflect on the One who set these laws?
